  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_4bd083c6-96a1-4222-bd94-544c8874dc05'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer15584665643983669983">Startling, unusual, and yet irresistably readable, <i>Among Others</i> is at once the compelling story of a young woman struggling to escape a troubled childhood, a brilliant diary of first encounters with the great novels of modern fantasy and science fiction, and a spellbinding tale of escape from ancient enchantment.<br/><br/>Raised by a half-mad mother who dabbled in magic, Morwenna Ph</span>
  <span id="freeText15584665643983669983" style="display:none">Startling, unusual, and yet irresistably readable, <i>Among Others</i> is at once the compelling story of a young woman struggling to escape a troubled childhood, a brilliant diary of first encounters with the great novels of modern fantasy and science fiction, and a spellbinding tale of escape from ancient enchantment.<br /><br />Raised by a half-mad mother who dabbled in magic, Morwenna Phelps found refuge in two worlds. As a child growing up in Wales, she and her twin sister played among the spirits who made their homes in industrial ruins, but her mind found freedom and promise in the science fiction novels that were her closest companions. When her mother tries to bend the spirits to dark ends with deadly results, Mori is sent away and must try to come to terms with what has happened without falling prey to the darkness.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15584665643983669983" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

Not every book is for everyone, cert<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Life is too short to read books you're not enjoying. Reading is supposed to be a pleasure, not an endurance test. Not every book is for everyone, certainly not every book of mine. Go read something else!<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_358327465" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/358327465" />
    <a title="Apatt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt"><img alt="Apatt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/358327465?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Apatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Apatt" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=fantasy-top20">fantasy-top20</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer358327465" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10218133007470591862">Now and then I come across a book that is a distillation of what I like in fiction, genre fiction in particular. I previously raved about <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14201.Jonathan_Strange___Mr_Norrell' title='Jonathan Strange & Mr Norrell by Susanna Clarke' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jonathan Strange & Mr Norrell</a> and then some people told me they think it's a load of ol' crap. It puzzled me a bit that some people don't see the greatness of the books I deemed to be great, but then I realize that such things probably puzzle most of us, we are all arbiters of good taste in our own little universe. So given that after reading this book you may</span>
  <span id="freeText10218133007470591862" style="display:none">Now and then I come across a book that is a distillation of what I like in fiction, genre fiction in particular. I previously raved about <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14201.Jonathan_Strange___Mr_Norrell" title="Jonathan Strange &amp; Mr Norrell by Susanna Clarke" rel="nofollow noopener">Jonathan Strange &amp; Mr Norrell</a> and then some people told me they think it's a load of ol' crap. It puzzled me a bit that some people don't see the greatness of the books I deemed to be great, but then I realize that such things probably puzzle most of us, we are all arbiters of good taste in our own little universe. So given that after reading this book you may not agree with my assessment of it (much to my astonishment) I am going into "rave mode" again.<br /><br />Firstly I am going steal SF Signal's one sentence synopsis:<br /><br /><i>"A teenaged girl in 1979 deals with her witch of a mother, faeries, a difficult boarding school life, and the joys of discovering science fiction and fantasy."</i><br /><br />Any mention of boarding school and fantasy in the same sentence will tend to trigger the name "Harry Potter" in people's minds, well you can fuggedaboudit, it's just an ordinary boarding school, no Defence Against the Dark Arts classes here. In fact, the setting of the boarding school resonates with me very much as a former pupil of such a school. It is a tough environment for geeky sci-fi loving kid that I was and Morweena— the protagonist and narrator of this book—is. The loneliness, the bad food, the discovery of like-minded friends all ring very true to me.<br /><br />From the synopses of this book at Goodreads, Amazon etc. fantasy fans are probably unsure whether this really is a fantasy novel at all and not just some rambling of a delusional girl. Well, the author has stated clearly in interviews that the fantasy element is not meant to be ambiguous, even if it may seem that way. You see, Jo Walton has done something very different with the so-called "magic system" trope here. In the universe of this book, the magic is very discreet and always has "plausible deniability" in that the effect of the magic may look like a normal coincidence. This makes the magic even more dangerous than in your average fantasy epic, the effect can be devastatingly wide ranging with everybody none the wiser about the cause. I am not going to give any example of this, it is really worth discovering by yourself.<br /><br />The most important aspect of this book is that it is a love letter to science fiction and fantasy books, I have never seen so many books and authors mentioned in a single book and they are mostly books I am very familiar with. Like Asimov's Foundation, Delany's Babel 17, Tolkien's LOTR etc. At the time the story is set, in late 1979 and early 1980 fantasy was not the massively popular genre it is today and the fantasy books were far outnumbered by the sf books, so interestingly this book is actually about a science fiction reader in a fantasy world. Most of the books mentioned are sci-fi classics with only the odd LOTR and Narnia books thrown in. The little comments about the books and the love the author via her characters show for the books make me want to read sf/f until my eyes fall out.<br /><br />The book is beautifully written in eloquent yet fairly simple prose in an epistolary format (diary entries), the characters are very well developed and believable. I can actually imagine what it feels like to be a teenage crippled girl in spite of my many disqualifications for identifying with such a character. As I understand it the story is partly autobiographical in that many of the key events are based on Ms. Walton's own experiences as a teenager. I found the climax to be oddly conventional in its spectacularity and it does not seem to conform with the relative quietude of the preceding chapters. Still, no real harm done.<br /><br />Jo Walton clearly loves the sf/f genre and reading in general with a passion, a feeling I share and this book is another one to be cherished.<br />A solid 5 stars for a well deserved 2011 Nebula Awards Winner.<br /><br />___________________________________<br /><br />Further reading:<br /><a href="http://io9.com/5922140/ask-jo-walton-anything-you-want-about-among-others" rel="nofollow noopener">Jo Walton's Q&amp;A at Io9</a><br /><a href="http://papersky.livejournal.com/509278.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Bibliography for Among Others</a><br /><a href="http://whatever.scalzi.com/2011/01/20/the-big-idea-jo-walton/" rel="nofollow noopener">Jo Walton's The Big Idea article</a><br /><br /><u>Update, September 4, 2012</u><br /><i>Among Others</i> just won the 2012 Hugo Award for Best Novel, a few months prior to that it won the Nebula Award. It has also been nominated for the World Fantasy Award for Best Novel. That should be enough accolades for anyone considering reading this book!</span>
  <a data-text-id="10218133007470591862" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_392934176" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/392934176" />
    <a title="carol. " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3978225-carol"><img alt="carol. " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307934801p2/3978225.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/392934176?book_show_action=true">Aug 15, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="carol. " class="user" itemprop="url" name="carol. " href="/user/show/3978225-carol">carol. </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">sci-fi fans</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to carol.  by: </span>
      the Incomparables
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=female-lead">female-lead</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=urban-fantasy">urban-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=hugo">hugo</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=nebula">nebula</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=awards">awards</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=magical-realism">magical-realism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer392934176" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12419285699388400380">A love letter to science fiction and fantasy, to books and to librarians:<br/><b><br/>"We never looked anything like anyone in our family, but apart from the eye and hair color I don't see anything. It doesn't matter. I have books, new books, and I can bear anything as long as there are books."<br/></b><br/>A support letter to adolescence, and to girls alienated from their families:<br/><b><br/>"So then I realised guiltily how my very presence in his car was actually a huge reproach. For one thing, there is only one of me, when he aba</b></span>
  <span id="freeText12419285699388400380" style="display:none">A love letter to science fiction and fantasy, to books and to librarians:<br /><b><br />"We never looked anything like anyone in our family, but apart from the eye and hair color I don't see anything. It doesn't matter. I have books, new books, and I can bear anything as long as there are books."<br /></b><br />A support letter to adolescence, and to girls alienated from their families:<br /><b><br />"So then I realised guiltily how my very presence in his car was actually a huge reproach. For one thing, there is only one of me, when he abandoned twins. For another, I am crippled. Thirdly, I am there at all; I ran away. I had to ask for his help--and worse, I had to use the social services to ask for his help. Clearly, the arrangements he made for us were far from adequate. In fact, my existence there at that moment demonstrated to him that he is a rotten parent."<br /></b><br />and to girls alienated from their schools:<br /><b><br />"If the school was going out of their way to try to detach us from magic, they couldn't organize things better. I wonder if that was someone's original intention... We don't have our own plates, or our own knives and forks or cups. Like most of what we use, they're communal, they're handed out at random. There's no chance for anything to become imbued, to come alive through fondness. Nothing here is aware, no chair, no cup. Nobody can get fond of anything."<br /></b><br />**************************************<br />Walton's writing is astonishing; it impeccably captures the voice of a 15 year old Welsh girl, Morwenna Markova, after she is sent to England to live with her estranged father and then immediately dispatched to boarding school. Books have been a life-long source of enjoyment and solace, and sustain her through the loss of her twin. She is both naive and worldly, with that bookish sort of experience that is not backed by the experience of real life:<b><br />"A Polish Jew! I am part Polish. Part Jewish? All that I know about Judaism comes from <i>A Canticle for Leibowitz</i> and <i>Dying Inside.</i> Well, and the Bible, I suppose."</b><br /><br />Fans of fantasy and science fiction will love the multitudes of references to genre classics, from LeGuin to McCaffrey to Zelazny and Vonnegut. Literary references are veiled--Mor and her sister call factory near them Mordor, as it <b>"looked like something from the depths of hell, black and looming with chimneys of flame"</b>--and overt--Mor frequently interprets the world according to authors she has read: <b>"Robert Heinlein says in <i>Have Spacesuit, Will Travel</i> that the only things worth studying are history, languages, and science."</b> Librarians become allies in Mor's adjustment to England, and through them, she discovers other like-minded souls.<br /><br />However, there is also something about the writing that is distancing, perhaps because of Mor's own emotional distance, perhaps because the narrative about the fairies is enigmatic, or perhaps because the overtones of fear in dealing with her mother that aren't realized. There is an awkward exploration of sexuality, perfectly age-appropriate, but uncomfortable, and relationships that doesn't quite weave in as smoothly as I would have liked. Whatever it was, it prevents me from that emotional connection that characterizes my five-star reads.<br /><br />Walton is an interesting writer, and is content to leave questions lying around, unanswered. If you are the sort of reader that likes wrapping with neat bows, this may not suit you. Still it is intriguing, and will undoubtedly win a second or third full read down the road.<br /><br />Quite a surprise, and one of the more unusual pieces I've read. Four and a half unreliable stars.<br /><br />Cross posted at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://clsiewert.wordpress.com/2013/03/07/among-others-by-jo-walton-or-a-love-letter-to-fantasy-readers/">http://clsiewert.wordpress.com/2013/0...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="12419285699388400380" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_288430940" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/288430940" />
    <a title="Lyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn"><img alt="Lyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495541962p2/5253785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/288430940?book_show_action=true">Mar 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer288430940" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4399044371303962866">This is one of those books that I think of frequently, it has stayed with me. <br/><br/>Walton has blessed this work with a strong female lead and is minimalistic, meaning that most of the action, what little there is, is subtle and underplayed. Yet it is a hypnotic book to read, Jo Walton does a great job of characterization, economically describing the cast in such a way that the reader knows the populace, yet there are few one dimensional characters. <br/><br/>Of course the aspect of the book that gets so much a</span>
  <span id="freeText4399044371303962866" style="display:none">This is one of those books that I think of frequently, it has stayed with me. <br><br>Walton has blessed this work with a strong female lead and is minimalistic, meaning that most of the action, what little there is, is subtle and underplayed. Yet it is a hypnotic book to read, Jo Walton does a great job of characterization, economically describing the cast in such a way that the reader knows the populace, yet there are few one dimensional characters. <br><br>Of course the aspect of the book that gets so much attention (rightfully so) is the ubiquitous references to science fiction literature. Akin to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/31712.Ernest_Cline" title="Ernest Cline" rel="nofollow noopener">Ernest Cline</a>'s novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9969571.Ready_Player_One__Ready_Player_One___1_" title="Ready Player One (Ready Player One, #1) by Ernest Cline" rel="nofollow noopener">Ready Player One</a>, the narrative at times takes a back seat to Walton's streaming sci-fi reminiscences. Whereas Cline took us back to the 80s, Walton waxes nostalgic through a list of science fiction and fantasy greats.<br><br>No wonder now, looking back, that this won the Hugo, it's that good.<br><br>** 2018 - This is a book for readers. Walton's ongoing list of cool SF books weaved into the storyline and the themes of reading akin to magical realism makes this a very special and fun book. I may need to re-read some time.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1568500395i/28149416._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="267" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="4399044371303962866" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_408174633" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/408174633" />
    <a title="Nataliya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya"><img alt="Nataliya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605995014p2/3672777.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/408174633?book_show_action=true">Sep 05, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nataliya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nataliya" href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya">Nataliya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/9863276-among-others">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2015-reads">2015-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=hugo-nebula">hugo-nebula</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2013-reads">2013-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2014-reads">2014-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer408174633" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12790930532570966176"><blockquote>
  <i>“This is for all the libraries in the world, and the librarians who sit there day after day lending books to people.”</i>
</blockquote>I'm not quite sure how to describe this book, what precisely it is - but it has that *something* that is making me read it for the third time in as many years, and each time it finds a new way into my heart.<b>
  <blockquote>
    <i>“There are some awful things in the world, it’s true, but there are also some great books.”</i>
  </blockquote>
</b>This is a <b>love ode to books and libraries</b>, and the magic of stories, </span>
  <span id="freeText12790930532570966176" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>“This is for all the libraries in the world, and the librarians who sit there day after day lending books to people.”</i>
</blockquote>I'm not quite sure how to describe this book, what precisely it is - but it has that *something* that is making me read it for the third time in as many years, and each time it finds a new way into my heart.<b>
  <blockquote>
    <i>“There are some awful things in the world, it’s true, but there are also some great books.”</i>
  </blockquote>
</b>This is a <b>love ode to books and libraries</b>, and the magic of stories, and the unashamed homage to so many science fiction classics, and the perfect understanding that <b>
  <i>"If you love books enough, books will love you back."</i>
</b><blockquote>
  <i>“Half way,” Glorfindel said, and he didn’t mean I was half dead without her or that she was halfway through or any of that, he meant that I was halfway through Babel 17, and if I went on I would never find out how it came out.<br>There may be stranger reasons for being alive.”</i>
  <br>
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1424891182i/13815928._SX540_.jpg" width="380" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</blockquote>It is also an offbeat story of a girl who grew up seeing fairies in Wales, <b>walking a thin line between magic and mundane</b> - or perhaps, just playing it all in her overactive and slightly unstable imagination.<blockquote>
  <i>“And it was the landscape that formed us, that made us who we were as we grew in it, that affected everything. We thought we were living in a fantasy landscape when actually we were living in a science fictional one.”</i>
</blockquote>It is a story about <b>adjusting to the life in which you are an outsider</b>, where you stick out like a sore thumb, an outsider still stripped raw from the death of the twin sister who was a part of you more than others could ever understand and from the shattering your life took both physically and emotionally.<blockquote>
  <i>"Twins are clones, too. If you looked at an elm tree you’d never think it was part of all the others. You’d see an elm tree. Same when people look at me now: they see a person, not half a set of twins."<br><br>“I have finished with saving the world, and I never expected it to be the slightest bit grateful anyway.”</i>
</blockquote>It's a story of learning to live with the physical and mental pain and learning to redefine a new normal for yourself, <b>burying all the possibilities from before and trying to focus on the realities of now</b>.<blockquote>
  <i>“It’s too late for that now. I’m going to grow up and she isn’t. She’s frozen where she is, and I’m changing, and I want to change. I want to live. I thought I had to live for both of us, because she can’t live for herself, but I can’t really live for her. I can’t really know what she’d have done, what she’d have wanted, how she’d have changed.”</i>
</blockquote>It's a story of <b>painful search and longing for people who are *yours*</b>, your <i>karass</i>, who will understand you and accept you and be there for you even when everything is crashing down around you.<blockquote>
  <i>"Being left alone—and I am being left alone—isn’t quite as much what I wanted as I thought. Is this how people become evil? I don’t want to be."<br><br>“Bibliotropic,” Hugh said. “Like sunflowers are heliotropic, they naturally turn towards the sun. We naturally turn towards the bookshop.”</i>
</blockquote>And <b>it's a story of the aftermath</b>, of what happens after climax of the 'before' story had been achieved and the survivors are left to pick up the pieces of their lives.<blockquote>
  <i>“[...] When I needed someone, somehow that net of family that I counted on to be there for me, the way you might bounce down to a trampoline, disappeared, and instead of bouncing back I hit the ground hard.”</i>
</blockquote>Don't look for much of conventional plot here - that's been left behind in the story that Mori only alludes to, the story after the climax of which we join in. This is Frodo's life after the Scouring of the Shire, as Mori notes.<blockquote>
  <i>“Tolkien understood about the things that happen after the end. Because this is after the end, this is all the Scouring of the Shire, this is figuring out how to live in the time that wasn’t supposed to happen after the glorious last stand. I saved the world, or I think I did, and look, the world is still here, with sunsets and interlibrary loans. And it doesn’t care about me any more than the Shire cared about Frodo.”</i>
  <br>
  <br>
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1424891182i/13815929._SX540_.jpg" width="380" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</blockquote>Don't look for conventional magic or fantasy or you'll be sorely disappointed. Mori's world is full of very vague, very subtle 'magic' that you can easily rationalize as either the remnants of magical thinking of childhood or perhaps a way her traumatized mind comes to rationalize the trauma that left her broken - or, if you want to be cruel, perhaps some of the seeds of madness that, if we believe Mori, possess her mother, the woman who persistently burns one of her daughters out of the photographs that she sends to her.<br><br>Don't be frustrated by the constant name-checking of endless pre-1980 science fiction and the entire journal entries comprised of little but Mori's precocious thoughts and impressions of them. <blockquote>
  <i>“One of the things I’ve always liked about science fiction is the way it makes you think about things, and look at things from angles you’d never have thought about before.”</i>
</blockquote>Yes, it does help if you have read at least some of them. <b>Yes, it's precisely the wealth of books that remains at the heart of this story about finding self in the world that is not rushing to meet you with open arms. </b>Yes, there will be more about books and Mori's impressions of them than any other plot strands. It's the strange beauty of this book, and it's the heart of it.<blockquote>
  <i>“Interlibrary loans are a wonder of the world and a glory of civilization.<br>Libraries really are wonderful. They’re better than bookshops, even. I mean bookshops make a profit on selling you books, but libraries just sit there lending you books quietly out of the goodness of their hearts.”</i>
  <br>
  <br>
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1424891182i/13815930.jpg" width="380" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</blockquote>Mori Phelps <i>(Morwenna? Morganna? You tell me, I'm still confused by the implications of a couple of seemingly throwaway and yet deeply significant lines in the book)</i> needs to pick up the pieces of her shattered life and learn to live with the aftermath, and not just simply live but be herself, find friends, thrive, find new things that matter, make new post-aftermath hopes and fears, come to peace with her losses and move on while still keeping what's dear to her in her heart.<br><br><b>And if sometimes the reasons to pick yourself back up and keep going are strange and unusual - well, so be it.</b><blockquote>
  <i>“And here I am, still alive, still in the world. It’s my intention to carry on being alive in the world, well, until I die. [...] I’ll live, and read, and have friends, a karass, people to talk to. I’ll grow and change and be myself. I’ll belong to libraries wherever I go. [...] Things will happen that I can’t imagine. I’ll change and grow into a future that will be unimaginably different from the past. I’ll be alive. I’ll be me. I’ll be reading my book.”</i>
</blockquote>Wonderful book, unusual, subtle and memorable, a tribute to the time of searching for books in the bookstores and libraries and not knowing what will come your way, and forever a tribute to the times of searching for yourself in life and really having no idea what will come your way. <br><br><b>5 stars.</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="12790930532570966176" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_140273587" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/140273587" />
    <a title="Nicky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/223837-nicky"><img alt="Nicky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308224598p2/223837.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/140273587?book_show_action=true">Jan 12, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicky" href="/user/show/223837-nicky">Nicky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/223837-nicky?shelf=queer">queer</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/223837-nicky?shelf=fairytales">fairytales</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/223837-nicky?shelf=welsh-writing">welsh-writing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/223837-nicky?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer140273587" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1840318788891887830">I tried to write this review without spoilers, but it depends on what you consider to be spoilers. I think it's a book based more on characters than events, and I don't think knowing some of the events will spoil the whole, but you might want to exercise a bit of caution...<br/><br/>Among Others feels like a book written just for me. The protagonist, Mori, is Welsh, disabled, synaesthetic, listens to folk music, reads SF and fantasy (reads anything and everything)... She says, early in the books, that, "I</span>
  <span id="freeText1840318788891887830" style="display:none">I tried to write this review without spoilers, but it depends on what you consider to be spoilers. I think it's a book based more on characters than events, and I don't think knowing some of the events will spoil the whole, but you might want to exercise a bit of caution...<br /><br />Among Others feels like a book written just for me. The protagonist, Mori, is Welsh, disabled, synaesthetic, listens to folk music, reads SF and fantasy (reads anything and everything)... She says, early in the books, that, "It doesn't matter. I have books, new books, and I can bear anything as long as there are books." She speaks casually and without bigotry about LGBT people, thinks about religion, and thinks positively -- at least mostly so -- about sex. She's at an English school, a private school with traditions and expectations and such a very middle class air. And her world is a world with room for all of this, for everything we know in our everyday lives, and also for magic. <br /><br />It's a book written after the climax of the story, really. Mori's fantastical adventures have come to their climax, and now she has to live with the consequences. She says that it's like life after the Scouring of the Shire. And she does have huge consequences to live with: she has her own injury, and the death of her twin sister, to come to terms with.<br /><br />It's a book about an ordinary girl, in many ways, and most definitely about an ordinary world. Magic comes through the cracks, but most of the time Mori has to live with things just the way we all do, catching buses and trains, and being excited about the latest books by her favourite authors coming out. And about being attracted to boys, too -- though in many ways this book is as much a love story about a girl and the interlibrary loan system as it is about a girl and a boy. It's a book about books, as much as anything else, maybe more than anything else. Mori talks about everything she's reading, often with astute comments about it all. I want to find and read some of these books, and try to find the same magic in them as the protagonist of Among Others does.<br /><br />There were two things I didn't connect with as well, and they don't detract from it enough to deduct a star, even though they seem to be the things I have the most to say about. One is that the final confrontations feel very abrupt. Part of that is Mori's matter of fact narration, and part of it is that it does come up very suddenly, after a lot of "real world" concerns and preoccupations -- it seems to jar against the rest, there. I would almost prefer the book without closure, without climax, because it is a story written after the world didn't end, and Mori is living with the consequences.<br /><br />The other thing, which I think is well articulated by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/144682372" rel="nofollow noopener">this review</a>, is that Mori's mother is a complete stereotype of a neglectful, mentally ill mother. As I said to that reviewer, part of that could be that Mori is only just learning about shades of grey -- it is in the last fifty pages or so that she says that children are better tools for fairies because they don't see in shades of grey; for most of the book, she doesn't see in shades of grey, she is in the process of learning to do so. Mori sees the world in the way it might be seen in children's fantasy -- a point she makes for herself about Susan Cooper's <i>The Dark is Rising</i> (although she is somewhat wrong: there are shades of grey in that sequence, too) -- where things are black and white, and there is a definite bad guy. She's fifteen years old, not an adult yet, and she is just growing toward a more nuanced view of the world. So I think it is partially that which informs the portrayal of her mother, and there are some subtle things, maybe that you only see when you know them from the inside, that hint at Mori's fears and the things her mother has done to her. The ones that I mentioned to the other reviewer are what particularly struck me: the way she disguises taking a book from the shelf, fearful of her mother's knowing, and also the way she says that she makes sure not to give anything away (at school), because it will be used against her. Those are thought patterns you get from being bullied/abused, in my experience.<br /><br />At the same time, it takes work to see the potential subtleties in the portrayal of Mori's mother -- it's all too easy to just take Mori at her word, and it is discomforting to wonder about how much of that is intentional and how much is just internalised by the author.<br /><br />Overall, though, I loved the book. I read it with my teddy, Helen, at all times, because it felt somehow wrong to read something that spoke to my teenage self without her -- this book really felt written for me, and I could talk about it for hours, if given the chance. One of my favourite things, though, was the very last line -- it feels almost bathetic, and yet at the same time, so perfect for Mori, so perfect for the story, and so perfect for me. The whole book was immensely easy to read, and I wish I'd been able to just set aside a few hours and blast right through it -- most of what happens in it is just ordinary life, but Mori's voice is well done and it's all quite magical, even the parts that aren't meant to be, because there's magic in reading and talking about reading and reading about reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1840318788891887830" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2739704125" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2739704125" />
    <a title="BlackOxford" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford"><img alt="BlackOxford" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484863606p2/17744555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2739704125?book_show_action=true">Mar 06, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="BlackOxford" class="user" itemprop="url" name="BlackOxford" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford">BlackOxford</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=fantasy-horror">fantasy-horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=biography-biographical">biography-biographical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=british">british</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2739704125" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17694672867269838434"><b>Bibliotropism</b><br/><br/>Excellent YA reading with acute relevance to us oldies as well. A fifteen year old girl from the Welsh valleys learns about life, death, sex, love and friendship. Handicapped in mysterious circumstances, estranged from her mother for equally mysterious reasons, Morwenna has to cope with everything from family blending to the trials of social isolation at an English girls’ school.<br/><br/>But mostly <i>Among Others</i> is about Morwenna’s irrepressible attraction to books, especially to the imaginat</span>
  <span id="freeText17694672867269838434" style="display:none"><b>Bibliotropism</b><br /><br />Excellent YA reading with acute relevance to us oldies as well. A fifteen year old girl from the Welsh valleys learns about life, death, sex, love and friendship. Handicapped in mysterious circumstances, estranged from her mother for equally mysterious reasons, Morwenna has to cope with everything from family blending to the trials of social isolation at an English girls’ school.<br /><br />But mostly <i>Among Others</i> is about Morwenna’s irrepressible attraction to books, especially to the imaginative construction of alternative worlds in sci-fi. These take up where her younger fantasies of faeries leave off. <i>“We thought we were living in a fantasy landscape when actually we were living in a science fictional one.”</i> Faeries after all are very knowledgeable but they can’t do much in the world of people, not without help. <br /><br />Morwenna loves fantasy but despises allegory. Things are what they are and are degraded by being made to stand for other things. <i>“Fiction’s nice. Fiction lets you select and simplify,” </i>she says. Fiction, in other words, explains things. It helps a person get from a magical view of the world of a child to the realism of adulthood, without the loss of one’s imagination, including one’s moral imagination: <i>“One of the things I’ve always liked about science fiction is the way it makes you think about things, and look at things from angles you’d never have thought about before.”</i><br /><br />Books are the centre of Morwenna’s existence: <i>“Sometimes it feels as if it’s only books that make life worth living.”</i> Her judgment has been honed by reading all the best writers and understanding what makes them the best. Through her reading she also finds others who are sympathetic to her tastes and ways of thinking. By understanding them she understands herself and her situation. It is not an overstatement to say that she is redeemed through her reading.<br /><br />Walton’s epigram for <i>Among Others</i> is a subtle mis-quote from Virgil’s Aeneid: <i>“et haec olim meminisse iuvabit,” </i>that is <i>“it will be a joy to remember these things some day.”</i> She has left out the important first word of Virgil’s original: ‘Forsan’ in Latin; ‘Perhaps’, in English. For Walton, there is no perhaps about Morwenna’s life. She will always find joy in what she has read and what it has done for her life.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17694672867269838434" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_163318131" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/163318131?book_show_action=true">Apr 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brooke" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brooke" href="/user/show/126262-brooke">Brooke</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/126262-brooke?shelf=2011">2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/126262-brooke?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer163318131" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3614579047805775709">I keep going back and forth between 2 and 3 stars. I should have really loved this book, but I found myself more annoyed than charmed. Although it appears we're supposed to take the main character's story at face value and believe that the magic and fairies and her evil mother are real, I found myself writing it all off as her way of coping with a more mundane unstable mother and car accident. I think what pushed me over the edge into disbelief was the scene with the aunts and the earrings. I me</span>
  <span id="freeText3614579047805775709" style="display:none">I keep going back and forth between 2 and 3 stars. I should have really loved this book, but I found myself more annoyed than charmed. Although it appears we're supposed to take the main character's story at face value and believe that the magic and fairies and her evil mother are real, I found myself writing it all off as her way of coping with a more mundane unstable mother and car accident. I think what pushed me over the edge into disbelief was the scene with the aunts and the earrings. I mean, what was that except some belligerent child who's playing make believe and throwing a tantrum? And we're supposed to believe she's 15! And as much as I love books, read and eat and breathe books, I wanted her to get her head out of them and be in the real world for a bit and have conversations that were real. There's something to say for being a well-rounded person with multiple interests. I realize I sound awfully harsh regarding a fictional girl whose twin just died; maybe it was the over-use of the word "brill" to describe everything she liked. You'd think someone who read so much would have a larger vocabulary!</span>
  <a data-text-id="3614579047805775709" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer523897824" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8735941417929579442">Morwenna grows up in Wales hanging out with faeries. Nothing extraordinary about it, loved the matter of fact telling and how they're precisely as I imagined they’d be. Illusive<i> “They’d moved in with the green things after people had abandoned them”</i> and unfathomable. Some are pretty little things with gossamer wings, others creatures ripped from the pages of Grimm’s Fairy Tales. <i> “Fairies tend to be either very beautiful or absolutely hideous” </i> <br/><br/>She's 15 with a ton of issues - a mother who’s crac</span>
  <span id="freeText8735941417929579442" style="display:none">Morwenna grows up in Wales hanging out with faeries. Nothing extraordinary about it, loved the matter of fact telling and how they're precisely as I imagined they’d be. Illusive<i> “They’d moved in with the green things after people had abandoned them”</i> and unfathomable. Some are pretty little things with gossamer wings, others creatures ripped from the pages of Grimm’s Fairy Tales. <i> “Fairies tend to be either very beautiful or absolutely hideous” </i> <br /><br />She's 15 with a ton of issues - a mother who’s crackers, the death of her twin, the appearance of her father who’d dropped into her life after years without a whisper, then shipped her off to an English boarding school. A misfit who walks with a cane &amp; talks funny, more comfortable in the company of faeries than people; immersed in the world of books, fantasy &amp; sci-fi in particular. Friendless &amp; lonely, she’s also clever and surprisingly grounded. <br /><br />A strange, gentle story that I should have loved and did for the most part. <b> “Away with the faeries” </b> a Gaelic phrase my parents used to describe me. Grew up lost in my own little world so coming across Morwenna felt a bit like finding a soul mate. I’m not daft, never actually spoke to any but whereas Santa left me cold I truly believed in faeries, spent hours hunting for them in neglected places, overgrown fields, derelict buildings. Books were a refuge for me as well, a pint-sized immigrant with a Glaswegian brogue that sent my Canadian classmates into hysterics every time I opened my mouth. <br /><b>cons:</b> Why oh why didn’t she stick with the style she began in, the subtlety of magical realism. Instead you get this outta left field epic ending, unnecessary and jarring. Dropped to <b> 3 ½ stars and rounded to 3 </b> <br /><br /><b>meanderings: </b> All the references to ‘obscure’ sci-fi &amp; fantasy novels whet my appetite. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3619.Roger_Zelazny" title="Roger Zelazny" rel="nofollow noopener">Roger Zelazny</a> top of my list to try. <br /><br /><b>memorable</b> <i>“I should never have tried to talk to that fairy. Let someone else do something about Dutch elm disease. It isn’t my problem.” </i> </span>

<div id="review_257211044" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/257211044" />
    <a title="Jay Z" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5153399-jay-z"><img alt="Jay Z" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1490054212p2/5153399.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/257211044?book_show_action=true">Jan 05, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jay Z" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jay Z" href="/user/show/5153399-jay-z">Jay Z</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5153399-jay-z?shelf=speculative">speculative</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer257211044" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4027065116349211219">i am at a total loss about how this book got published. this is not a book! in the sense of a novel, i mean. it's the diary of an articulate, well-read sci-fi diehard teenager talking about what she did and what she read every day for a year. this account of her every day life is sprinkled with unimpressive encounters with fairies, because apparently in this world, welsh people are kinda down with spirit creatures. <br/><br/>the other reason this isn't a book is that it's an epilogue to a story we never g</span>
  <span id="freeText4027065116349211219" style="display:none">i am at a total loss about how this book got published. this is not a book! in the sense of a novel, i mean. it's the diary of an articulate, well-read sci-fi diehard teenager talking about what she did and what she read every day for a year. this account of her every day life is sprinkled with unimpressive encounters with fairies, because apparently in this world, welsh people are kinda down with spirit creatures. <br /><br />the other reason this isn't a book is that it's an epilogue to a story we never get to hear. mori, our main gal, is living with a father she barely knows and in boarding school having fled from her evil witch (literal, not metaphorical) mother. why? well, sometime before mori's boring daily diary begins, there was a huge magical battle between good (mori &amp; her twin sister) and evil (the mother), which left mori with a bad leg and a dead sister. you'd think that at least some of the book would explain why the mother was evil, why she hated her kids, why she waged magical war on them, and just generally, what kind of magic we're talking about. but nada. no explanations of any sort -- for ANYTHING. <br /><br />instead, this is what we get: school sucked today. my classmates are so lame. today, my mother tried to kill me in my sleep. that sucked, too. my leg hurts. i went to the woods and talked to a fairy. but they're really cryptic. wow, ursula le guin's awesome. this lesbian is hitting on me but i'm straight. how can anyone think heinlein is authoritarian? i wish i knew my father were better. i touched this guy's dick at a party and he called me a slut so guess i won't be seeing him again. my grandad's pretty cool though. i miss my sister but i want to live. let me go re-read lord of the rings for the six hundreth time. sci-fi book club, yay! my aunts keep trying to get my ears pierced. but you can't see fairies if your ears are pierced. i think my aunts are evil. <br /><br />i am not exaggerating. this is the entire book. WTF. i'm really kind of bitter because i kept a journal growing up. maybe if i added the occasional gnome on the side, i could get that published, too?</span>
  <a data-text-id="4027065116349211219" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_571385060" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/571385060" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/571385060?book_show_action=true">Mar 26, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=fanboy-goes-squee">fanboy-goes-squee</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=top-one-hundred">top-one-hundred</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer571385060" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6191126823036022713">I know what I was expecting before I had read this novel. I knew it had won last year's Hugo and I've been working my way through every hugo and runner up since they started. What I hadn't expected was an unabashedly delightful review of so many great science fiction novels from the last 75 years, most of which I've also read and delighted over. I loved Mor and was always very proud of her, and who wouldn't be? As long as you are a science fiction fan, using magic as easily as breathing, thinkin</span>
  <span id="freeText6191126823036022713" style="display:none">I know what I was expecting before I had read this novel. I knew it had won last year's Hugo and I've been working my way through every hugo and runner up since they started. What I hadn't expected was an unabashedly delightful review of so many great science fiction novels from the last 75 years, most of which I've also read and delighted over. I loved Mor and was always very proud of her, and who wouldn't be? As long as you are a science fiction fan, using magic as easily as breathing, thinking magic as easily as reading, you and I are her. She is our Everyman. <br /><br />So, yes, I do normally hit a lot of ratings at the five star level, but is that because I research novels before I read them and only aim for the best? Or is it because I always find something brilliant in everything I read? Maybe both, but I don't care. I love books, I love books, I love books... and my kinship with Jo Walton, even though I have never spoken with her, or even read any of her other novels, is one of karass. Thank You, Jo!<br /></span>

<div id="review_99155864" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/99155864" />
    <a title="Sherwood Smith" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/162084-sherwood-smith"><img alt="Sherwood Smith" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543007644p2/162084.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/99155864?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sherwood Smith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sherwood Smith" href="/user/show/162084-sherwood-smith">Sherwood Smith</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/162084-sherwood-smith?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer99155864" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4800479052788545631">When I first finished Jo Walton’s to Among Others, there was this instinctive pang of hurt at being left out because when I met Walton in Tempe for World Fantasy a few years back, she didn’t tell me about the fairies.<br/><br/>A heartbeat later my reasoning brain is sending the “Hello, this is fiction!” memo, but there it was, that delicious (and painful) sense of my having lived in that fictional world, the reading experience was so intense: it's the liminal existence I went to books for ever since I was</span>
  <span id="freeText4800479052788545631" style="display:none">When I first finished Jo Walton’s to Among Others, there was this instinctive pang of hurt at being left out because when I met Walton in Tempe for World Fantasy a few years back, she didn’t tell me about the fairies.<br /><br />A heartbeat later my reasoning brain is sending the “Hello, this is fiction!” memo, but there it was, that delicious (and painful) sense of my having lived in that fictional world, the reading experience was so intense: it's the liminal existence I went to books for ever since I was a little kid.<br /><br />The word "liminal" comes from the Latin limen, or threshold. For Victor Turner and a bunch of anthropologists of the latter part of the 20th Century, the liminal figure exists on the threshold of two worlds, and can partake of both. Turner and his associates studied cultural liminality—including marginalization and outsiders—and that segued into studies of the liminal periods of human existence, focusing on adolescence as a liminal state.<br /><br />Some regard artists, writers, and musicians as liminal, looking at social forms from the outside. If that’s true, maybe that’s one of the reasons why young adult literature is going through such an amazing popularity right now: writers and artists look at culture, especially (liminal) adolescent culture, from the outside. There are interlocking rings of liminality here . . . .<br /><br />. . . but that’s another discussion. Back to<i> Among Others.</i><br /><br />The storyline goes something like this. Some time after the accident that claimed the life of her twin sister Morganna, Morwenna Phelps is sent to live with her father and aunts. They put her in boarding school, which she hates; reading and journal writing are her only solace. Oh yes, and magic. Armed with these three things, she slowly begins to make sense of the world as she ages toward emancipation.<br /><br />The book opens with the girls doing some magic to get rid of the polluted sump of the factories. As nature reclaims the area and fairies return, the reader, trying to impose a sense of familiarity if not reality over the story, might be reminded of painful historical notes wherein polluted places are associated with beatific visions, another form of liminality.<br /><br />Like <i>A Separate Peace</i> and <i>To Kill a Mockingbird, Among Others</i> straddles that threshold between young adult and adult literature. All three view the adult world from the perspective of a young protagonist, not jut the kid world. Young adult novels are largely concerned with teen matters, and interactions with adults tend to be bounded by YA tropes. YA boarding school stories tend to follow rules set down more than a hundred years ago; though Mor attends a boarding school—one complete with a long history, and includes “Hons” among its students—her narrative is the antithesis of the boarding school story. The school and its world are not all-important. Mor is looking outside both figuratively and metaphorically.<br /><br />Not only is Mor right in the middle of that liminal stage of adolescence, her very identity is liminal: she and her sister shared the nicknames “Mor” or “Mori”, and once she uses her sister’s name; she leaves Wales as Phelps but is enrolled under her father’s name, Markova; she’s liminal culturally, being Welsh in England, she’s liminal, or marginalized, as she can’t participate in games, that boarding school megalith, she reads science fiction, which not only socially marginalizes her, it enables her to view the world by comparing it to these fictional worlds, a uniquely liminal perspective.<br /><br />Her journal is curiously liminal, reading most of the time like a journal (written in “mirror”), but everyone so often she talks to someone outside of herself: <i>My family is huge and complex, and perfectly normal in all ways. It’s just—no. If I think about trying to explain it to somebody well-meaning who doesn’t know anything about it, I’m daunted in advance.</i><br /><br />These are not the only liminal identities. Mor’s mother; the three aunts whom Mor can’t tell apart; her paternal grandfather, who had the most precarious liminal existence. By introducing us to these characters, the narrative engages the reader with the liminality of life.<br /><br />There is the liminal nature of magic.<i> It is difficult to define: You can never be sure where you are with magic. And you can never be sure if you’ve really done anything or if you were just playing.</i><br /><br />From the diffuse to the details of everyday living, magic is liminal:[At boarding school]<i> Still, on the subject of eating, we don’t have our own plates, or our own knives and forks or cups. Like most of what we use, they’re communal, they’re handed out at random. There’s no chance for anything to become imbued, to come alive through fondness. Nothing here is aware, no chair, no cup. Nobody can get fond of anything. At home I walked through a haze of belongings that knew, at least vaguely, who they belonged to. Grampar’s chair resented anyone else sitting on it as much as he did himself.</i><br /><br />Magic, its possibility (or probability), its nature and its dangers become a powerful thread in the story. There are also the fairies, whose liminality is striking:<i> I’ve always noticed how much more fairies are like plants than anything else. With people and animals you have one standard pattern: two arms, two legs, one head, a person. Or four legs and wool, a sheep. Plants and fairies, thought, there are signs that say what they are, but a tree might have a number of branches, growing out anywhere. There’s a kind of pattern to it, but one elm tree won’t look exactly like the next.</i><br /><br />Mor is also aware of the liminality of history:<i> The places of my childhood were linked by magical pathways . . . we gave them names but we knew unquestioningly that the real name for them was “dramroads.” I never turned that word over in my mouth and saw it for what it was: Tram road. Welsh mutates initial consonants. Actually, all languages do, but Welsh does it while your mouth is still open. Tram to dram, of course. Once there had been trams running on rails up and down those dramroads, trams full of iron or coal. So empty and leaf-stewn, used by nobody but children and fairies, they’d once been little railroads.</i><br /><br />Finally there is Mor’s reading, which is largely (though not exclusively) science fiction and fantasy. That’s a liminal genre right there. Mor talks about the novels she reads, sometimes reassessing them as she gets older; she finds like-minded people who talk books.<br /><br />Could younger people read it? You bet. They are most likely to not get any of the sf references, which mostly cover books that came out in the seventies or before, but when does that stop the smart reader? I remember encountering unfamiliar references at age twelve, when I first began exploring the adult shelves, and being stimulated to go searching for the hidden meaning. And in those days (banging cane) there was no Internet. But the library, I already knew, was filled with veins of treasure waiting to be explored.<br /><br />When I said that Mor engages with the adult world, some readers might ask if that means references to adult matters. As always, I encourage adults with curious reader kids to read it first. Mor talks about such matters as sex (including a somewhat harrowing close call) with the exact same combo of pragmatism and curiosity that I remember my fellow young teens talking about it when we were safely out of earshot of adults, and the same way I’ve heard students talk when I could hear them outside the open window of my classroom. Or how my kids talk to each other, when their voices echo up the stairwell.<br /><br />As Mor gets older, she discovers her personal boundaries blurring as much as the social boundaries. How she looks at the books she reads, how she compares their incidents and paradigms with her own experience, how she finds a group at last and what it means to be inside . . . how she deals with attraction and all its invisible assumptions and demands, and then there is how she deals with evil.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4800479052788545631" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_323488725" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/323488725" />
    <a title=" Danielle The Book Huntress (Back to the Books)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books"><img alt=" Danielle The Book Huntress (Back to the Books)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1403239075p2/1570669.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/323488725?book_show_action=true">May 03, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title=" Danielle The Book Huntress (Back to the Books)" class="user" itemprop="url" name=" Danielle The Book Huntress (Back to the Books)" href="/user/show/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books"> Danielle The Book Huntress (Back to the Books)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">SF/F fans and bibliophiles</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=witch-sorcereress-healer">witch-sorcereress-healer</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=hero-or-heroine-limps-disabled">hero-or-heroine-limps-disabled</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=historical-fantasy">historical-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=library-checkout">library-checkout</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=faerie">faerie</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=set-in-uk">set-in-uk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=epistolary-narrative">epistolary-narrative</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=2013-200s-club-challenge">2013-200s-club-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=family">family</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=school-days">school-days</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer323488725" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15812276956086848682">Among Others is kind of like a love letter to bibliophiles, especially those who fell in love with books as a youngster, finding solace and comfort between the pages of so many different stories. In some ways, Mor's character tapped me on the shoulder, reminding me of myself as a preteen. I went through some physical problems that made life very difficult for me. In fact, <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[ I had a hip problem that caused me to walk funny and had to use crutches before and after surgery, and people</span></span>
  <span id="freeText15812276956086848682" style="display:none">Among Others is kind of like a love letter to bibliophiles, especially those who fell in love with books as a youngster, finding solace and comfort between the pages of so many different stories. In some ways, Mor's character tapped me on the shoulder, reminding me of myself as a preteen. I went through some physical problems that made life very difficult for me. In fact, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ I had a hip problem that caused me to walk funny and had to use crutches before and after surgery, and people accused me of faking, as if you'd fake an injury so you could have attention you really didn't want. I also remember sitting in the library during gym hour (which was awesome since gym was always my least favorite class because all the bullies seemed to be in gym class). It was one of the few things I liked about having my hip problem, that and having a couple months out of school. I hated school, not the books but the system. But most of all, being in one of my favorite places in the world for an uninterrupted hour of reading. Whatever good books I could find in the school library. The possibilities were great, if not exactly endless, because I did eventually run out of books that I wanted to read.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> I also identified with how Mor saw her life through the lens of fiction. I think that people who spend so much time reading do tend to analyze life and books in that manner.<br /><br />I found myself wanting to write down all the book titles, and even looked some up on my Kindle Fire as I read. I am not a heavy science fiction reader, but I did read tons of fantasy and some sci-fi when I was younger. This book makes me want to investigate sci-fi with a renewed interest. It seems to have much to offer Mor, and perhaps I will find the same appeal with further reading. As Mor did, I read all the ones my library had, and then some of the adult books at that point. I remember that joy, which I still have, of going to the library and bookstores and finding what new books I could read. There never seemed to be enough books. The identification factor was very strong with Mor in this regard. Also having divorced parents, and how that opens a wound inside you that doesn't ever seem to heal. Lastly, a sister I love dearly. Now, my mom wasn't an evil witch. Nor was I gifted with magic powers and the abilities to see fairies (although I would love to see fairies, to be honest. I guess I'm on the wrong side of the ocean for that).<br /><br />In some ways, this book has a surreal flavor. Many times I wondered how much of Mor's magic-sensing abilities and magical frame of reference was just part of her imagination's way of dealing with some events that a young person doesn't know how to handle. But, then, I think that there is too much reality to the magic here to come to that conclusion, ultimately. At any rate, I liked how at times you couldn't tell. <br /><br />This lovely book is a piece of fiction that feels so intimate and personal to me. I can only believe that the author poured her own love of books and some of her own experiences with books into this book. That kind of intense realness cannot be faked. Books are such a pleasure, one that never pales. You can find so much joy and pathos in a book that it literally is like opening a door to another world, where you can escape from your own little problems enough to gain courage to face another day. Whether that's a school full of mean girls, or parents who fight more than they show affection. Or physical problems, loss, loneliness, you name it. As an adult, that allure of books hasn't palled for me. I like to think that a grown up Mor finds just as much joy and solace in her books. And I can't fault her for it. I'm the same way.<br /><br /><b>Overall rating: 4.5/5.0 stars</b><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15812276956086848682" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
