    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1105930262" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1105930262" />
    <a title="Ferdy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4596358-ferdy"><img alt="Ferdy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1487898103p2/4596358.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1105930262?book_show_action=true">Nov 14, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ferdy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ferdy" href="/user/show/4596358-ferdy">Ferdy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=wtfery">wtfery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=dead-people">dead-people</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=awful-love-interest">awful-love-interest</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=disappointing">disappointing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=boring">boring</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=paedophiles">paedophiles</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=shitty-parents">shitty-parents</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=magic">magic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=insufferable-mary-sue-heroine">insufferable-mary-sue-heroine</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1105930262" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15911975179491103989"><b>Spoilers</b><br/><br/>I mostly disliked this, the main character was an irritating twit, the plot consisted of a mish-mash of nothing, and then there was the weird WTF ending. I did however like the magical system/elements (up until the end), the book-talk (well, some of it), the setting/descriptions of Wales, and how fast it was to read.<br/><br/>-What's what: Teenager and speshul snowflake, Mori, is reunited with her estranged dad and sent to a boarding school after some unexplained Bad Thing happened to her and her </span>
  <span id="freeText15911975179491103989" style="display:none"><b>Spoilers</b><br /><br />I mostly disliked this, the main character was an irritating twit, the plot consisted of a mish-mash of nothing, and then there was the weird WTF ending. I did however like the magical system/elements (up until the end), the book-talk (well, some of it), the setting/descriptions of Wales, and how fast it was to read.<br /><br />-What's what: Teenager and speshul snowflake, Mori, is reunited with her estranged dad and sent to a boarding school after some unexplained Bad Thing happened to her and her now dead twin sister. Her evil mother somehow caused the Bad Thing, she wanted power or world domination or something (I'm not quite sure). <br />There's also fairies and magic which only Mary Sue Mori and a couple of others can see, the fairies are bloody useless and tell Mori to do random things to help nature or the world or something (again not quite sure). <br />Most of the book though consists of Mori banging on about how smart she is, her boring school life, and the various mostly obscure/old SFF books she's read/reading.<br /> <br />-I wasn't impressed with the diary format, it was poorly done and felt fake. Some of the entries were believably written but others not so much. There were too many entries which contained info-dumps and linear/perfectly remembered dialogue/conversation - they didn't read like diary entries at all.<br /><br />-What was with the point of giving the life history of Mori's extended family? There were so many paragraphs and descriptions of her relatives, I thought they would be important somehow but they weren't, they were mentioned the once and then never mentioned again. Everything about them was utterly irrelevant.<br /><br />-Didn't much like Mori's character, she was stuck up, self involved, and judgemental (though she claimed she wasn't). She treated the girls at her boarding school like rubbish, she looked down at them, acted crazy around them and tried to scare them. Yet she had the nerve to be upset when they weren't fawning over her and being friends with her. She didn't make any effort with the girls at her school, she pretty much hated all of them even the ones she thought of as friends. Did she ever consider that she was the problem and not them? Nope, they were all bitches and she was the innocent one. Ugh.<br />The more I read about Mori, the more I dislked her, she was so unsympathetic. She was a like a robot most of the time, she was more or less indifferent to her supposedly beloved twin sister's death. She had more emotions and thoughts about a guy she knew for a few weeks, it was so unrealistic. Where was her grief, anger and loss at her sister's death? They were meant to be close but it was hard to tell with the way Mori went on with her life. She didn't need to be in tears but she could have at least showed some emotion when it came to her sister.<br /><br />-I was disappointed that the climactic and most significant event which happened in Mori's life occurred before the book even began. <i>Among Others</i> was basically a look into what comes after the end of an epic story/battle. I was waiting to find out why Mori was the way she was, how she came to be where she was, and what the Big Thing that happened to her actually was. But it was never really explained, it was referenced loads but there was no detail or real insight given. It was the most significant thing that ever happened to her, it apparently changed her, and lead her to where and who she as at the start of the novel yet the author felt no need to give proper answers or detail into that part of her life. Why leave out the most interesting aspect of the main character?! <br /><br />-There were way too many references to old/classic sci-fi/fantasy books, I hadn't heard of most of them. Whenever they were brought up and used to make a point or a comparison it went over my head and left me a little lost.<br />I did enjoy the general bookish tidbits like the book shopping, the library parts, the MC's love for reading, but I wasn't impressed with the mentions of the more obscure SFF books, especially when they were being used to illustrate a point.<br />Also, I found it hard to believe how much Mori was able to read, she read dozens of hefty books in a matter of days, she couldn't possibly have had that much free time. <br /><br />-I was disgusted at Mori's dad (Daniel) wanting to have sex with her and then Mori brushing of the almost rape and incest as nothing. Her reaction was bizarre. Why wasn't she disturbed or afraid? It was like it was no big deal for a dad to come on to their teenage daughter. Not only that there was a part where Mori actually considered having sex with him and regretted telling him no. Where did that come from?! Then it was all forgotten about and never mentioned again, it was unreal how it didn't affect Mori's relationship with her dad in the slightest. It was even more infuriating that by the end Daniel was made out to be a good guy, as if it was perfectly fine for him to get drunk, slip in his daughter's bed and try to have sex with her. It was shrugged off as if it was no big deal for a dad to want/try to have sex with their teenage daughter, as if it was just a silly mistake and as if dads that do that are essentially good guys. Ugh, I hated how the almost rape/incest was handled, it was glossed over and written in such a light manner.<br /><br />-I thought things couldn't get any worse after the dad thing but it did, the story turned into a cheesy YA romance in the latter half, with Mary Sue Mori getting the attention of Wim - the hottest, baddest, sluttiest guy ever. Of course, all the other girls Wim dated were as Wim put it 'morons' who he didn't actually like but was happy to fuck. Naturally, Mori was different to all the other girls, ugh. Why would Mori even want Wim when he treated Ruthi and all his other ex's like rubbish? He dumped Ruthi when he though she might be pregnant. Why would Mori be cool with that when her own dad abandoned her? It made no sense. I hated Wim and Mori's relationship, I didn't sign up to read a crappy YA romance.<br /><br />-Was the mum truly evil or what? Or did Mori just think of her that way because of her mental illness? What was the mum trying to actually achieve? Power? Control? Revenge? And how did Mori defeat her the first time? Did the rest of the family know about the mother or their powers? How did they even get powers in the first place? How did they learn about magic? Who taught them?<br /><br />-Why did Mori jump to the random conclusion that her aunts were witches? They gave her silver earrings on Christmas and were planning on taking her to get her ears pierced and from that Mori deduced they were witches. Why would she make that random connection? It was like she just made up magical rules based on nothing but her whim.<br /><br />-There was a made up word (karass) from another SFF book that was used over and over again. I didn't know what it meant, I had to search online to find out its proper meaning. It was only towards the end of the book when it'd already been used dozens of times that the author bothered to explain its meaning. What was the point of doing that after using it throughout the rest of the book with no explanation? It should have been done much earlier, the word 'karass' was after all the most important aspect to one of the arcs.<br /><br />-WTF was with the ending? Magic up until then had been subtle, unseen and ambiguous. But when Mori was facing her mum there was suddenly all sorts of flashy magic that was being used. Mori was able to make fire and trees and crazy images - she had never done that before, but suddenly she was able to conjure up loud, showy magic. It didn't fit with the rest of the book, it was better when the magic/fairies were more ambiguous. <br /><br />All in all, it wasn't for me. There were too many elements which frustrated me: the insufferable heroine, the awful romance with Wim, all the boring school stuff and the many reviews/mentions/references of books I'd never heard of. <i>Among Others</i> would probably appeal more to hardcore SFF lovers.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15911975179491103989" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_949261995" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/949261995" />
    <a title="B Schrodinger" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5448792-b-schrodinger"><img alt="B Schrodinger" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572325701p2/5448792.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/949261995?book_show_action=true">May 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="B Schrodinger" class="user" itemprop="url" name="B Schrodinger" href="/user/show/5448792-b-schrodinger">B Schrodinger</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/12349140-among-others">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5448792-b-schrodinger?shelf=books-about-books">books-about-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5448792-b-schrodinger?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer949261995" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14878812439709757907">Is <i>Among Others</i> the story of a girl who can use magic, talk to faeries and has a wicked witch for a mother? Or is it the story of a girl who has a abusive mother, a father who ran away when she was a baby and whose twin died in a tragic car accident only a year before?<br/><br/>When we join Morwenna's diary she is being shuffled off to her estranged father's house after an incident with her mother. She hobbles with a walking stick and can tell that everyone thinks she is a cripple. She has never met her f</span>
  <span id="freeText14878812439709757907" style="display:none">Is <i>Among Others</i> the story of a girl who can use magic, talk to faeries and has a wicked witch for a mother? Or is it the story of a girl who has a abusive mother, a father who ran away when she was a baby and whose twin died in a tragic car accident only a year before?<br /><br />When we join Morwenna's diary she is being shuffled off to her estranged father's house after an incident with her mother. She hobbles with a walking stick and can tell that everyone thinks she is a cripple. She has never met her father before, nor his three sisters who she suspects to be three magic users themselves, but not good magic. But she finds a fellow reader in her father who has an extensive SF collection that she adores. <br /><br />The three aunts pay for Morwenna to attend boarding school and which she attends with minimal worry. In a way it is better than being in the same house as her aunts. At school she makes very few friends, but finds some fellow souls in the form of a SF reading group at the local library.<br /><br />This is a very low key book plot-wise, it is more a coming of age book. Morwenna comes to find new love and friendship that she never expected to meet away from her home. There is no doubt that she is a very emotionally and even physically damaged person, and past events are discussed at certain parts in the book. But Jo does a brilliant job of dancing the line between truth and fiction in a way that is always on the side of the fantasy being true, but not enough for you to discount the whole diary as the warped reality of a damaged teenager. You are enchanted so much that you are willing to believe that Morwenna can converse with the faeries and that magic does occur in her life. <br /><br />There is no doubt that this book is as charming as they come and is filled with love for books and the understanding of people who just don't fit in. But it is much more. The characters are distinct and three-dimensional and their motives are well known. There is also a lot of nostalgia to be had being set in 1980. Here was a book lover who may not know about a favourite author's new work until she managed to stumble across it in a book store in the city. Or who relies on the limited supplies of the local library, the school library and the second hand bookshop in town. There certainly is no more romantic book hunting in the age of ordering online from any title in print and having it on your doorstep a couple of days later, or even of ebooks. <br /><br />I read the book a couple of years ago when the paperback edition first came out and I was sceptical. I'm not the type to like a book about girls who talk to faeries. But i fell in love. So this time I listened to the audiobook which was read by a Welsh lady. It definitely added a whole new spin on the story. Her voice was so distinctive. <br /><br />So I'd recommend this to all book lovers, especially those of you out there who spent their youths trawling second-hand bookstores in the pre-internet age looking for that book in that series that you loved, or the out of print graphic novel that you saw once in the schoolyard.<br /><br />To borrow from Morwenna "I thought it was brilliant". </span>
  <a data-text-id="14878812439709757907" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_966915233" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/966915233" />
    <a title="Diane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/279256-diane"><img alt="Diane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1497365060p2/279256.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/966915233?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane" href="/user/show/279256-diane">Diane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780765331724" href="/book/show/11669085-among-others">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Diane by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/5448792-b-schrodinger">B Schrodinger</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=bookishness">bookishness</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer966915233" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer352246744153782535">This novel was a delight. I fell in love with the precocious Morwenna, a Welsh girl whose life was turned upside down after her twin sister died. Morwenna was sent to live with her father in England, and then ends up at boarding school.<br/><br/>Morwenna is a bright girl who loves to read, and her opinions about books were some of my favorite parts of the novel. (She loves science fiction, which I haven't read a lot of, but I enjoyed her enthusiasm.) Morwenna has trouble making friends at school, but even</span>
  <span id="freeText352246744153782535" style="display:none">This novel was a delight. I fell in love with the precocious Morwenna, a Welsh girl whose life was turned upside down after her twin sister died. Morwenna was sent to live with her father in England, and then ends up at boarding school.<br /><br />Morwenna is a bright girl who loves to read, and her opinions about books were some of my favorite parts of the novel. (She loves science fiction, which I haven't read a lot of, but I enjoyed her enthusiasm.) Morwenna has trouble making friends at school, but eventually she finds her own group when she joins a sci-fi book club at the town library.<br /><br />So far, this novel sounds normal, right? That's because I haven't mentioned the fairies, or magic, or the fact that Morwenna's mom is a witch who may or may not have tried to kill her and her sister. <br /><br />Morwenna believes in fairies and can practice magic with their help. I loved this paragraph from the opening chapter:<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <br />
  <i>Think of this as a memoir. Think of it as one of those memoirs that's later discredited to everyone's horror because the writer lied and is revealed to be a different colour, gender, class and creed from the way they'd made everybody think. I have the opposite problem. I have to keep fighting to stop making myself sound more normal. Fiction's nice. Fiction lets you select and simplify. This isn't a nice story, and this isn't an easy story. But it is a story about fairies, so feel free to think of it as a fairy story. It's not like you'd believe it anyway.</i>
  <br />
</blockquote><br /><br />I listened to this on audio, which was excellent. It was performed by Katherine Kellgren, who did beautiful Welsh and English accents. This book earns my highest compliment, which is that I didn't want it to end. I wanted the story to keep going. I wanted to watch Morwenna grow up and continue to hear about her reading and to find out what happens with her and the cute boy. I generally don't read series, but I hope someday the author writes a sequel.<br /><br />I think anyone who likes bookish stories and/or smart girls would appreciate this novel. I highly recommend it.<br /><br />(Many thanks to GR friend Brendon for recommending this gem of a novel.) <br /><br /><b>Favorite Quote</b><br />"Interlibrary loans are a wonder of the world and a glory of civilization. Libraries really are wonderful. They're better than bookshops, even. I mean bookshops make a profit on selling you books, but libraries just sit there lending you books quietly out of the goodness of their hearts."</span>
  <a data-text-id="352246744153782535" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_368487863" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/368487863" />
    <a title="Kevin Xu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2310290-kevin-xu"><img alt="Kevin Xu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1390443151p2/2310290.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/368487863?book_show_action=true">Jul 14, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kevin Xu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kevin Xu" href="/user/show/2310290-kevin-xu">Kevin Xu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer368487863" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6195417788028251265">There are few problems I had with the book. First, as far as fantasy goes, I'm not really a big fan of the magical realism type of fantasy like this book is, I like to be transported into a new world with great world building. Second, I'm also not a big fan of geek nostalgia like this or <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9969571.Ready_Player_One' title='Ready Player One by Ernest Cline' rel='nofollow noopener'>Ready Player One</a>. Third, I thought there could have been more to the book than basically about discovering the genre.<br/><br/>The book is about a fifteen year old girl who gets send to boarding school after the death of </span>
  <span id="freeText6195417788028251265" style="display:none">There are few problems I had with the book. First, as far as fantasy goes, I'm not really a big fan of the magical realism type of fantasy like this book is, I like to be transported into a new world with great world building. Second, I'm also not a big fan of geek nostalgia like this or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9969571.Ready_Player_One" title="Ready Player One by Ernest Cline" rel="nofollow noopener">Ready Player One</a>. Third, I thought there could have been more to the book than basically about discovering the genre.<br /><br />The book is about a fifteen year old girl who gets send to boarding school after the death of her twin by her father. It is basically seen though her diary as so goes to school from 1979-1980, and finds herself discovering all the classic science fiction/fantasy, but mostly science fiction since fantasy was still growing though the inter-library loan at her local library then discussing it at the library with the science fiction book club on Tuesdays, which is in part autobiographical. <br /><br />This book is partly autobiographic of the author, and to many fantasy/science fiction lovers of the genre including me, which what makes up most of the book. The discovering/introduction to the genre when one is a teenagers, then trying to read all the genre has to offer in one sitting. I did that when I got out of high school, discovering all the new books by using the inter-library loan at my university to get all the books I found. Also this is the story of how one can be awkward during the teenage years in the process of discovering what is the real reality of life/world.<br /><br />I read this book because there are many discussions about how great this book was by many in the science fiction/fantasy community, and it even won this year's Nebula, and its nominated for the Hugo. I don't see/understand the hype in the book.<br /><br />After all this what one positive thing I have to say about the book is that it is a great way for people to introduced to the genre because there are many titles of books within, even though its out of date, and mostly science fiction because of the time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6195417788028251265" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_235095063" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/235095063" />
    <a title="Sue" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3642045-sue"><img alt="Sue" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1274829293p2/3642045.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/235095063?book_show_action=true">Nov 13, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sue" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sue" href="/user/show/3642045-sue">Sue</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer235095063" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10567859922862436715">WOW! This really was something. It is the daily journal of a young teenage girl, surviving twin daughter of a Welsh witch, a girl living in 1979 Britain with the father she did not know and about to be sent off to a boarding school "among others".<br/><br/>This young 15 year old is both younger and older than her classmates and is above all a reader, a passionate book lover....especially works of science fiction and fantasy. These books keep her soul alive when life is too hard and come to bind her to oth</span>
  <span id="freeText10567859922862436715" style="display:none">WOW! This really was something. It is the daily journal of a young teenage girl, surviving twin daughter of a Welsh witch, a girl living in 1979 Britain with the father she did not know and about to be sent off to a boarding school "among others".<br /><br />This young 15 year old is both younger and older than her classmates and is above all a reader, a passionate book lover....especially works of science fiction and fantasy. These books keep her soul alive when life is too hard and come to bind her to others.<br /><br />I would recommend this book to anyone who enjoys those areas of reading. This is also a very different take on developing responsibility, individuality and maturity (if that doesn't sound too stuffy, for this is anything but a stuffy book).<br /><br />I'm going to have to read it again to make note of all the books it references. Of course there are Tolkein and Asimov, Heinlein, Dick, C.S.Lewis, etc. But there's also Plato! and Hitchhiker's Guide to the Galaxy. <br /><br />And Mori speaks such tributes to libraries and librarians!! They should be published in newspapers. I guess I'm in love with this book right now. Thank you, Jo Walton.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10567859922862436715" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_515413868" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/515413868" />
    <a title="Stephanie *Extremely Stable Genius*" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1085121-stephanie-extremely-stable-genius"><img alt="Stephanie *Extremely Stable Genius*" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1563066276p2/1085121.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/515413868?book_show_action=true">Jan 23, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephanie *Extremely Stable Genius*" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephanie *Extremely Stable Genius*" href="/user/show/1085121-stephanie-extremely-stable-genius">Stephanie *Extremely Stable Genius*</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1085121-stephanie-extremely-stable-genius?shelf=2013">2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1085121-stephanie-extremely-stable-genius?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1085121-stephanie-extremely-stable-genius?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1085121-stephanie-extremely-stable-genius?shelf=books-about-books">books-about-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer515413868" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10678380003777968478"><i>"I care more for people in books than the people I see every day.”</i><br/><br/>uh....kind of the truth sometimes.<br/><br/>Morwenna Phelps is a fifteen year old who is a veracious reader, especially for anything Science Fiction, or 'SF' as she calls it. I was tempted to write down every book mentioned as this book moved along, but seriously that would have been more work than I really had the energy to do.<br/><br/>I'm not sure how to review this book without putting out a spoiler or two. I'm going to try to avoid it, but....<br/><br/>Am</span>
  <span id="freeText10678380003777968478" style="display:none"><i>"I care more for people in books than the people I see every day.”</i><br /><br />uh....kind of the truth sometimes.<br /><br />Morwenna Phelps is a fifteen year old who is a veracious reader, especially for anything Science Fiction, or 'SF' as she calls it. I was tempted to write down every book mentioned as this book moved along, but seriously that would have been more work than I really had the energy to do.<br /><br />I'm not sure how to review this book without putting out a spoiler or two. I'm going to try to avoid it, but....<br /><br />Among Others is written in the first person in the form Morwenna's diary, which begins after a horrible car crash that both Morwenna and her twin are in. Mor's leg is severely injured and her twin doesn't survive. Mor takes refuge in her books to get though it and heal.<br /><br /><i>“It doesn't matter. I have books, new books, and I can bear anything as long as there are books.”</i><br /><br />Morwenna and her sister grew up seeing fairies and doing magic (very vague magic). Mor's mother is a very powerful and evil witch, for whom she blames for her twins death. Mor runs away from her mother to stay with a father who she has never met and is sent off to boarding school. <br /><br />Everything that happens in this book, or diary, is all filtered through Morwenna and her SF books. Morwenna IS her SF books, so what might actually be happening and what Mor is telling us are likely two different things altogether. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I kept expecting that it would come out that Mor had a mental break after the crash and the loss of her sister, and that all the belief in magic and fairies were a part of it <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br /><i>“I don’t think I am like other people. I mean on some deep fundamental level. It’s not just being half a twin and reading a lot and seeing fairies. It’s not just being outside when they’re all inside. I used to be inside. I think there’s a way I stand aside and look backwards at things when they’re happening which isn’t normal.”</i><br /><br />I really enjoyed Among Others, a unique read, written in a style that I I truly liked. I haven't come across anything quite like this book. It doesn't give you many answers, it doesn't tie things up nice and neat, and I liked that. <br /><br /><i>“This isn’t a nice story, and this isn’t an easy story. But it is a story about fairies, so feel free to think of it as a fairy story. It’s not like you’d believe it anyway.”</i><br /><br />Also posted at <a href="http://www.shelfinflicted.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Shelfinflicted</a><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="10678380003777968478" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer413239814398530835">meh.<br/><br/>this books rambles along for a couple hundred pages, and then things happen on the last few. for me, by the time i got to the last few pages, i was pretty much just hoping to finish this book and be done with it so i could go on to the next book (a new collection of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4920740.Thurber_James' title='Thurber James' rel='nofollow noopener'>Thurber James</a>.<br/><br/>the book is written in the form of an adolescent bibliophile's diary. that form gives it a plus: the narrator's voice, unadulterated. in this case the narrator's voice is very clear, distinctive, with an unusual and</span>
  <span id="freeText413239814398530835" style="display:none">meh.<br /><br />this books rambles along for a couple hundred pages, and then things happen on the last few. for me, by the time i got to the last few pages, i was pretty much just hoping to finish this book and be done with it so i could go on to the next book (a new collection of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4920740.Thurber_James" title="Thurber James" rel="nofollow noopener">Thurber James</a>.<br /><br />the book is written in the form of an adolescent bibliophile's diary. that form gives it a plus: the narrator's voice, unadulterated. in this case the narrator's voice is very clear, distinctive, with an unusual and uncommonly independent point of view. the form also gives the book its downside: it rambles, it's repetitive, and it often lacks a larger, comprehensive vision, being as it is mired in the day-to-day.<br /><br />the other plus and minus to this book is that it refers to scads of early SF books. it's fun when you know the book she's writing about, when you are an insider. it's not so fun when you don't know the book or haven't read it for 30 years.<br /><br />to me, this book was a love poem to the joy of reading. but that fine aim doesn't rescue it from the mundanities of who the narrator is buying buns for at the boarding school, or how many trains one has to take to get from A to B. </span>

<div id="review_335255214" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/335255214" />
    <a title="Wanda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9246169-wanda"><img alt="Wanda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528903039p2/9246169.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/335255214?book_show_action=true">May 23, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wanda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wanda" href="/user/show/9246169-wanda">Wanda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=female-authors">female-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=read-in-2014">read-in-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=speculative-fiction">speculative-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=bechdel-test">bechdel-test</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=march-2021">march-2021</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer335255214" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5846613637020744402">As a soldier in the army against ignorance (a library worker), how could I help but be charmed by this book? It is, in many ways, a testament to the ways that libraries and librarians can make a difference in people’s lives. I was astonished last year when I realized that I had now worked 30 years in the library field, but looking back it should not have been a big surprise. I clearly remember the thrill that I got on the first day that I was allowed into the “big kids’ library” in our small tow</span>
  <span id="freeText5846613637020744402" style="display:none">As a soldier in the army against ignorance (a library worker), how could I help but be charmed by this book? It is, in many ways, a testament to the ways that libraries and librarians can make a difference in people’s lives. I was astonished last year when I realized that I had now worked 30 years in the library field, but looking back it should not have been a big surprise. I clearly remember the thrill that I got on the first day that I was allowed into the “big kids’ library” in our small town school. The satisfaction of finding books that engaged and moved me—starting simply with Walter Farley’s Black Stallion series and culminating when I discovered H. Rider Haggard and J.R.R. Tolkien. <br /><br />That is the second reason that <i>Among Others</i> charmed me—I have read science fiction all of my reading life and have appreciated its way of getting me to look at my world from new angles. I have been working my way chronologically through a long list of classic science fiction and fantasy for the last three years and am just moving into the 1980’s decade, so many of the works referenced in <i>Among Others</i> were fresh in my mind. In fact, I was reading Heinlein’s <i>The Number of the Beast</i> at the same time that I was reading about Morwenna’s discovery of it as a newly published work. <br /><br />I’m sure many adolescents go through the phase of feeling like they don’t fit in and struggling to find people with whom they click—and it’s very difficult in a rural or small town situation, where numbers of children in your own age group are limited, so I could relate to Morwenna’s struggle. I was also fortunate as a farm child to have access to a university extension program—we received regular boxes of books, containing what the university librarians considered appropriate for children, including loads of fairy tales, Greek mythology, and classic books. I especially appreciated the mythology and longed to see centaurs, Pegasus, and dryads for myself, but these seemed to be delicate Mediterranean creatures that did not frequent the Canadian prairies. I didn’t encounter Mary Renault’s work back then, but I think that is an omission that I will have to correct, as Morwenna and I share similar tastes.<br /><br />I love how involved we can become in these fictional works—like Morwenna, I can always count on LOTR to completely immerse me, despite the number of times I have taken that journey. I treasure the books that I can read repeatedly and happily, as well as those that deprive me of sleep because I simply can’t set them down.<br /><br />Recommended for library and book lovers, science fiction readers, and those who grew up in rural surroundings (especially if you are all of the above).<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5846613637020744402" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_415662290" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/415662290" />
    <a title="RandomAnthony" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/721021-randomanthony"><img alt="RandomAnthony" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470919631p2/721021.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/415662290?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="RandomAnthony" class="user" itemprop="url" name="RandomAnthony" href="/user/show/721021-randomanthony">RandomAnthony</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer415662290" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1036348542598563174">I liked the hell out of <i>Among Others</i> in part because the novel makes me feel like I <i>belong</i>. This is my tribe. All that. And I don't feel like I belong often. So yay, Ms. Walton! You deserve all the awards you can stuff in your bulging suitcases. That said, I can't recommend this book if you're not one of the tribe. What tribe? The nerdy science fiction reading tribe. If your membership has lapsed you'll probably still enjoy <i>Among Others</i>, don't worry, but if you've not felt (sadly, proudly, or a </span>
  <span id="freeText1036348542598563174" style="display:none">I liked the hell out of <i>Among Others</i> in part because the novel makes me feel like I <i>belong</i>. This is my tribe. All that. And I don't feel like I belong often. So yay, Ms. Walton! You deserve all the awards you can stuff in your bulging suitcases. That said, I can't recommend this book if you're not one of the tribe. What tribe? The nerdy science fiction reading tribe. If your membership has lapsed you'll probably still enjoy <i>Among Others</i>, don't worry, but if you've not felt (sadly, proudly, or a mix of both) socially inept and found solace in science fiction at one point or another, read another book. I don't intend to sound mean, but goddammit, all you other people got to kiss in middle school and probably didn't know the local librarians by name and, in retrospect, didn't feel their pitying gaze as you read by the window three nights a week. So leave this book for us. Okay? Thanks. Fuckers. I recognize that some of you might love science fiction AND functioned well socially, but I don't know what to make of you, so I'm not speaking to you. <br /><br /><i>Among Others</i> focuses on a teenage girl who lands, after watching her twin sister die and running away from her evil witch mother, at her distant father's house. In turn her aunts ship her off to a decidedly not-Hogwarts boarding school. She has a limp, and she's Welsh, neither of which apparently add to one's popularity in these scenarios. The novel takes the form of the girl's journals. Shut up, if you groaned I'm kicking your shins. Morwenna (the girl) is smart but not faultless, observant but not always accurate. And she reads. A lot. And she writes about books in her journal, real books, I mean, at least some of which even casual science fiction readers will recognize. I'm partial to the Zelazny she cites (esp. the Amber books) but disagree with her PK Dick dismissal. Eventually Mor (who can see fairies) either finds or creates (your call) a science fiction club and meets a boy who may or may not be worth her time. Still, even with the “mysterious boy” angle, if a librarian/bookstore clerk were to place this book next to the shopworn paranormal teen romances that person should be fired. This book belongs (there's that word again) on a different shelf. I'm not sure <i>Among Others</i> has a start-to-finish plot as much as a dreamy, fascinating progression. The book's strengths lie in Morwenna's voice and the assumption that the reader gets all the insider literary references. Instead of rendering science fiction palatable to the outsider, Ms. Walton gives the finger to readers who can't keep up. Or maybe, more positively phrased, she creates a conversation respectful of lifelong readers. I don't mean to sound snotty. I'm more thrilled to feel that sense of belonging. Everyone's invited. But not everyone takes up the invitation. <i>Among Others</i> is a blast, and the last few pages build to a moving, beautiful finish. Well done, Ms. Walton. I'm proud to know you. I hope I'm not impertinent in feeling I do. Secret handshakes all around.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1036348542598563174" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_308458748" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/308458748" />
    <a title="Kris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5974610-kris"><img alt="Kris" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1390152276p2/5974610.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/308458748?book_show_action=true">Apr 07, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kris" href="/user/show/5974610-kris">Kris</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5974610-kris?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer308458748" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5631336525318492167">"If you love books enough, books will love you back."<br/><br/>I found this book, written in the form of a diary, to be a lovely, nostalgic read. Mori is an endearing protagonist who is struggling to find a place for herself after a tragic magical battle left her twin sister dead and her estranged from her mother. As Mori struggles to maintain her identity at a snobbish boarding school, she turns to books for comfort. Although I know that some readers found her frequent references to SF and fantasy works </span>
  <span id="freeText5631336525318492167" style="display:none">"If you love books enough, books will love you back."<br /><br />I found this book, written in the form of a diary, to be a lovely, nostalgic read. Mori is an endearing protagonist who is struggling to find a place for herself after a tragic magical battle left her twin sister dead and her estranged from her mother. As Mori struggles to maintain her identity at a snobbish boarding school, she turns to books for comfort. Although I know that some readers found her frequent references to SF and fantasy works off-putting, I loved Walton's ability to capture all the ways that much-beloved books speak to truths more real than any held by the superficial material world. (It is, of course, possible that I am projecting here - Mori's relationship to books reminds me of mine, especially when young.) <br /><br />I don't want to give any spoilers, so I will make this brief. Walton's book speaks to an adolescent's search for a deep connection with others, be they family members or true friends who ask the same questions of books and life. Mori struggles with many uncertainties about why others might like her - she worries about the ethics of using magic to find friends, as she fears the hollowness of a forced connection. Among Others captures Mori's progress to adulthood, with all the uncertainties she faces along the way. Throughout, she emerges as a courageous heroine who holds true to herself and her values, even when facing peer pressure, physical pain, emotional insecurity, and even magical battles with her mother. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5631336525318492167" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText17606479484868829829" style="display:none">I am of two minds about this book. There are things about it I liked a great deal. And then there were the ways in which I reached the end and went "huh." Let's see if I can sum up - I like the authorial voice and the way the world Walton creates is dripping with books. On the other hand, the pacing is strange, and rarely if ever does what comes seem to build on what's gone before. I mean, it happens after previous events. It isn't that they are forgotten, but the book doesn't tend to use what has come before to construct a satisfying narrative.<br /><br />Note: The rest of this review has been withdrawn due to the changes in Goodreads policy and enforcement. <div id="review_268535622" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/268535622" />
    <a title="seak" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1805180-seak"><img alt="seak" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1410456529p2/1805180.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/268535622?book_show_action=true">Jan 27, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="seak" class="user" itemprop="url" name="seak" href="/user/show/1805180-seak">seak</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1805180-seak?shelf=arc-review">arc-review</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1805180-seak?shelf=2013">2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1805180-seak?shelf=audio">audio</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer268535622" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer961900665152633713">This book got rave reviews when it came out, even won the Nebula Award, which I can see. However, I didn't absolutely love it as seems to be the norm.<br/><br/>What I loved:<br/><br/>I did love a few things. I loved that I really wanted to go back and read all the classics of science fiction and fantasy. Walton writes with such a love of the genre that you can't help but become enamored yourself. <br/><br/>I loved that if you are a fantasy nerd like me and the main protagonist Mori (and it may be safe to assume, Jo Walton), </span>
  <span id="freeText961900665152633713" style="display:none">This book got rave reviews when it came out, even won the Nebula Award, which I can see. However, I didn't absolutely love it as seems to be the norm.<br /><br />What I loved:<br /><br />I did love a few things. I loved that I really wanted to go back and read all the classics of science fiction and fantasy. Walton writes with such a love of the genre that you can't help but become enamored yourself. <br /><br />I loved that if you are a fantasy nerd like me and the main protagonist Mori (and it may be safe to assume, Jo Walton), then you can completely relate to living two lives. One in a fantasy land you never want to leave and one in the "real" world where you don't quite completely fit in ... whatever that means.<br /><br />What I thought was more three stars out five:<br /><br />This was really a love note to SFF with a story at the far background, if any. It's obviously there, but only mildly mentioned and then all of a sudden some stuff happens in the end. <br /><br />I think I expected more of a blend of the two; references to classic SFF stories and a story itself as opposed to more of the former and less of the latter. <br /><br />It's still a decent story, but not really a classic. I see why it won the Nebula, it's essentially a love letter to the Nebula Award and, therefore, a dead-ringer from the get-go, but it didn't quite work for me.<br /><br />I'll leave with a couple great quotes for your nerdy heart:<br /><blockquote>"Interlibrary loans are a wonder of the world and a glory of civilization."</blockquote><br /><blockquote>"Glory Road is deeply disappointing ... I love Heinlein but he clearly doesn't get fantasy. It's just stupid." (I felt the exact same way reading Glory Road. Starship Troopers is amazing, but I don't know if I'll attempt his fantasy again)</blockquote><br />3 out of 5 Stars (Recommended with reservations)</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2342372252" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2342372252" />
    <a title="Allison Hurd" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14495279-allison-hurd"><img alt="Allison Hurd" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530670583p2/14495279.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2342372252?book_show_action=true">Apr 10, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Allison Hurd" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Allison Hurd" href="/user/show/14495279-allison-hurd">Allison Hurd</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14495279-allison-hurd?shelf=sff-bookshelf">sff-bookshelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14495279-allison-hurd?shelf=sff-2018-challenge">sff-2018-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14495279-allison-hurd?shelf=fem-author">fem-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14495279-allison-hurd?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2342372252" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6412310532074982035">A charming coming of age story. A love letter to girls whose childhoods were spent half on Earth and half between the pages of whatever book they held. A remarkable allegory for living life that requires imagination to endure.<br/><br/><b>CONTENT WARNING</b>: (no actual spoilers, just a list of topics) <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[death of a sibling, car accidents, molestation of children (honest, not exploitative and not dwelled on), emotional abuse.  <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br/><br/><u>Things to love</u><br/><br/>-<b>Ugh. Everything</b>. Everything! Love this book </span>
  <span id="freeText6412310532074982035" style="display:none">A charming coming of age story. A love letter to girls whose childhoods were spent half on Earth and half between the pages of whatever book they held. A remarkable allegory for living life that requires imagination to endure.<br /><br /><b>CONTENT WARNING</b>: (no actual spoilers, just a list of topics) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[death of a sibling, car accidents, molestation of children (honest, not exploitative and not dwelled on), emotional abuse.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><u>Things to love</u><br /><br />-<b>Ugh. Everything</b>. Everything! Love this book and despair. No, I guess that's not actually helpful? Okay, I'll try to say more, but really, everything.<br /><br />-<b>Morwena</b>. She's brilliantly written. She IS the every girl. Sure, maybe not all of us were crippled in car accidents or have had to reestablish relationships with absent parents, but the way her life is a series of book quotes and figuring out philsophies she's read mixed with the mundane dramas of young adulthood were absolutely endearing. You wanted to hold her and say "I understand. It gets better, and you're remarkable." <br /><br />-<b>All the books</b>. I haven't read all of the books referenced, but enough that I caught on and while I don't think you needed them, they added a level to the thoughts/dialogue that felt like...well, having Goodreads, actually. It felt like talking to a friend who knows you so well they get the subtext without needing more said about it. Or, to use Anne Shirley, like having a kindred spirit.<br /><br />-<b>The honesty</b>. Loss. Pain. Awkwardness in social circumstances. Casual misogyny and abuse that are all weathered with just frustration and confusion, because what else is there? Again, it made me want to hold her and say how wrong it was that people did that, but that we were working on it. I loved that while there was some trauma, it wasn't really dwelled upon. Sometimes, you're a victim and sometimes you were a victim, and now have to get on with life. I felt this was done exceptionally well. I'm adding it to my list of books that tackle hard things in a respectful way, one that says "I see you" rather than "do you see me?"<br /><br />-<b>The magic allegory</b>. It's either a magic system pulled straight from fairytale, or a convenient way to process trauma. Which is it? Can it be both? Either way, it was beautiful and plucked at my heart strings.<br /><br />-<b>The writing</b>. This is a "story about nothing." And yet it felt to me like a story about everything. Masterfully composed, the voice of a lonely teen navigating things she ought not have to but penned with the careful attention of a protective and poetic adult.<br /><br />-<b>The audio</b>. The narrator was perfect, able to do all the different GB accents that made it feel even more immediate.<br /><br /><u>Things not to love:</u><br /><br />-This book is not a hack and slash or a romp. It's something I could see reading in high school English class. If you're expecting drama and magic, this isn't it. It's subtler--rocking in the lap of the book instead of tumbling down a hill after it.<br /><br />I can't believe this is my first Jo Walton, but I can assure you it won't be my last. Absolutely enamored.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6412310532074982035" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1263134568?book_show_action=true">Apr 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Helene Jeppesen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Helene Jeppesen" href="/user/show/21992464-helene-jeppesen">Helene Jeppesen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1263134568" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer11325372216480636694">I loved this book to pieces! Simply because it made me feel so good inside while reading it - it hit my heart spot on. <br/>I love that this is what I would call "light" fantasy mixed with magical realism that contains fairies. I also absolutely adored the main character, Mor, and the way she feels excluded from everyone and also has a limp. Mor was amazing in so many ways: Her reading habits were phenomenal and made me want to read a lot as well. She also made me want to visit my local library a lot</span>
  <span id="freeText11325372216480636694" style="display:none">I loved this book to pieces! Simply because it made me feel so good inside while reading it - it hit my heart spot on. <br />I love that this is what I would call "light" fantasy mixed with magical realism that contains fairies. I also absolutely adored the main character, Mor, and the way she feels excluded from everyone and also has a limp. Mor was amazing in so many ways: Her reading habits were phenomenal and made me want to read a lot as well. She also made me want to visit my local library a lot more than I already do. I absolutely adored the setting of the boarding school she attends even though it's portrayed as a cold, desolate and lonesome place. However, that only made her trips to the town library and the café so much better. Every time Mor had to go home to her father I wanted for her to go back to this magical town of books, coffee and intertextuality :) <br />This book comes with a lot of references to other books which I found interesting. Only once was I partly spoiled for a book that I might want to read later on in life, but it didn't matter because actually it only made me want to read that book even more. Mor has an amazing hunger for books and I could identify a lot with her. <br />I do see why some people would be disappointed in this story if they were expecting a magnificent fantasy story. I wasn't expecting anything and maybe that's why I ended up loving it so much. During the last pages I was dreading the end because I didn't want to leave Mor, and that's a sign that this is a great story worth reading!</span>
  <a data-text-id="11325372216480636694" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer15189695246726397521"><b>“... though why anyone would read any of them when the world contains all this SF is beyond me.“ </b><br/><br/>This is me from the age of 13 on. So I can savely say that this book was extremely relatable to me. A girl who lives for SF books, is a bit ‚other‘ and doesn’t easily find friends – what is not to love there? I don’t always agree with Mori’s choice of favourite authors (I’m more team Wim in this), but this gorgeous book spoke very much to me.<br/><br/>It is a light read. Despite the gloomy background of the pr</span>
  <span id="freeText15189695246726397521" style="display:none"><b>“... though why anyone would read any of them when the world contains all this SF is beyond me.“ </b><br /><br />This is me from the age of 13 on. So I can savely say that this book was extremely relatable to me. A girl who lives for SF books, is a bit ‚other‘ and doesn’t easily find friends – what is not to love there? I don’t always agree with Mori’s choice of favourite authors (I’m more team Wim in this), but this gorgeous book spoke very much to me.<br /><br />It is a light read. Despite the gloomy background of the protagonist it sparks of positivity and entertains with lovely mundane scenes out of the boarding school life of a disabled bookworm who can talk to fairies. Told in the way of diary records the book keeps the reader always close to the protagonist. One simply has to feel with and for Mori and be happy for the small victories she acquires in her everyday struggle. <br /><br />It is a quiet book, a feel-good book. Jo Walton succeeds in giving her protagonist a very distinctive voice and, like she did with the other 4 books of her that I’ve read, she convinces with a feathery, effortless prose which makes reading her works so utterly enjoyable.<br /><br />Last but not least – which SF reader doesn’t love to read a novel where there are SF books mentioned on every other page and one goes „Ah, I know this“ or „Ah, I should add this to my TBR“ all the time. This was a lot of fun.<br /></span>

<div id="review_471465006" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/471465006" />
    <a title="Jill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1788674-jill"><img alt="Jill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1432902126p2/1788674.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/471465006?book_show_action=true">Dec 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jill" href="/user/show/1788674-jill">Jill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1788674-jill?shelf=disappointment">disappointment</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1788674-jill?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer471465006" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2540477529823688562">To decide whether or not to read this book, answer this question: do you want to read about a 15 year old girl reading science fiction?<br/><br/>In my case, the answer is no. Even if I did adore classic SF from the mid-20th century, I don’t think I would have liked this book. It’s a diary format, covering a year of a young Welsh girl’s life at a drab English boarding school. Mori’s recovering from trauma—her sister is dead and her mom is evil and the reason for these facts has something to do with fairies</span>
  <span id="freeText2540477529823688562" style="display:none">To decide whether or not to read this book, answer this question: do you want to read about a 15 year old girl reading science fiction?<br /><br />In my case, the answer is no. Even if I did adore classic SF from the mid-20th century, I don’t think I would have liked this book. It’s a diary format, covering a year of a young Welsh girl’s life at a drab English boarding school. Mori’s recovering from trauma—her sister is dead and her mom is evil and the reason for these facts has something to do with fairies but it’s all very vague. Her recovery is helped by reading gobs and gobs of SF, which she discusses in her journals.<br /><br />It’s certainly very realistic. Journaling this way seems like something a precocious 15 year old girl would do, existence of fairies or not. But realistic doesn’t always make for enjoyable reading. The bits I was interested in were glossed over, probably because Mori used her diary as a therapeutic outlet to avoid retelling her exciting but troublesome magical problems. As a reader of the diary, however, I feel gypped after slogging through pages of expository writing on SF books only to gain a rare unexplained mention of magic/death/evil. The actual exciting events are told in the past tense without pizazz or, even worse, they’re not recounted at all, merely tantalizingly alluded to.<br /><br />The most interesting part of <i>Among Others</i> concerns the verisimilitude of it all. The common reading seems to be 1. magic exists 2. all these supernatural events did truly occur. But the diary format allows Mori to edit. Who knows how factual her account really is? Mori draws some odd conclusions about seemingly innocuous things <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I’m thinking about how she concludes her aunts are witches after they give her a pair of earrings for Christmas <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, and I’m tempted to read the book as the babblings of a lunatic girl pushed toward the fantastical after being punished by the realistic. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2540477529823688562" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2449492610" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2449492610" />
    <a title="Dawn C" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14948447-dawn-c"><img alt="Dawn C" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535435406p2/14948447.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2449492610?book_show_action=true">Apr 18, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dawn C" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dawn C" href="/user/show/14948447-dawn-c">Dawn C</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14948447-dawn-c?shelf=7th-read-all-the-books-challenge">7th-read-all-the-books-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14948447-dawn-c?shelf=media-audible">media-audible</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14948447-dawn-c?shelf=group-shelf">group-shelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14948447-dawn-c?shelf=loved">loved</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14948447-dawn-c?shelf=media-kindle">media-kindle</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2449492610" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17279312894123921286"><i>If you love books enough, books will love you back.</i><br/><br/>This is one of the best books I’ve ever read, in any capacity. A complete joy and utter delight from start to end. The novel is written as the meticulous diary of our main character, 15 year old Morwenna, or Mori for short, in her idiocyncratic, flowy, whimsical stream-of-consciousness way of talking, which is both liberatingly fresh and unselfconsciously funny in this completely unaware way.<br/><br/>Mori is a huge science fiction and fantasy enthusiast,</span>
  <span id="freeText17279312894123921286" style="display:none"><i>If you love books enough, books will love you back.</i><br /><br />This is one of the best books I’ve ever read, in any capacity. A complete joy and utter delight from start to end. The novel is written as the meticulous diary of our main character, 15 year old Morwenna, or Mori for short, in her idiocyncratic, flowy, whimsical stream-of-consciousness way of talking, which is both liberatingly fresh and unselfconsciously funny in this completely unaware way.<br /><br />Mori is a huge science fiction and fantasy enthusiast, and spends much of her time prowling libraries and bookshops for SFF, an interest she shares with her estranged father. The contrast between her passionate, rambling opinions on this or that piece of literature, only to immediately switch to a throwaway comment on something completely mundane is hilarious. Mori is one of the clearest personalities I’ve encountered in a book, with a young mind innocently hungry for knowledge and not yet weighed down by an adult’s sense of responsibility.<br /><br />She’s also on a personal level deeply relatable to me. I wish I had had her fighting spirit when I was her age, though!<br /><br />It’s also a story with faeries, the Welsh variety, and a girl who can see them, a girl whose mother is a witch and killed her sister, and whom the girl has run away from and uses wards and spells to protect herself against. Or it’s a story of a girl who imagines all of this to cope with the death of her sister. My favorite kind of magical realism is when you don’t know if the magic or the fantasy is real, or is just subjective to the character’s experience, and could work perfectly either way. Jo Walton hits the mark here.<br /><br />That it’s narrated so wonderfully with a Welsh sing-song accent by Katherine Kellgren is an added bonus. I adore Welsh and am a language nerd at heart, so I spent a lot of time pausing the audio to read the lines back aloud to myself as Welsh sounding as I could muster (thank god no one could hear me!). I bought the ebook as well, just for that purpose, and I’ll be keeping the audiobook so I can relisten &lt;3<br /><br />There’s a thousand things I love about this book, but I’ll end with a favorite quote:<br /><br /><i>I did not buy a book called Lord Foul’s Bane by Stephen Donaldson, which has the temerity to compare itself, on the front cover, to “Tolkien at his best.” The back cover attributes the quote to the Washington Post, a newspaper whose quotations will always damn a book for me from now on. How dare they? And how dare the publishers? It isn’t a comparison anyone could make, except to say “Compared to Tolkien at his best, this is dross.”</i><br /><br />😂</span>
  <a data-text-id="17279312894123921286" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('bbq+2yjCUkud8AmycZtp/GSt+tuY7mvjwpb7+ZJf5zcZoBZ1p00BWgYPiZX4YYO8NgQp4s2w5cETCaTy0o37vQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

But when the three become unexpectedly trapped in 1940, they struggle not only to find their way home but to survive as Hitler’s bombers attempt to pummel London into submission. Now the situation has grown even more dire. Small discrepancies in the historical record seem to indicate that one or all of them have somehow affected the past, changing the outcome of the war. The belief that the past can be observed but never altered has always been a core belief of time-travel theory—but suddenly it seems that the theory is horribly, tragically wrong. Meanwhile, in 2060 Oxford, the historians’ supervisor, Mr. Dunworthy, and seventeen-year-old Colin Templer, who nurses a powerful crush on Polly, are engaged in a frantic and seemingly impossible struggle of their own—to find three missing needles in the haystack of history. Told with compassion, humor, and an artistry both uplifting and devastating, All Clear is more than just the triumphant culmination of the adventure that began with Blackout. It’s Connie Willis’s most humane, heartfelt novel yet—a clear-eyed celebration of faith, love, and the quiet, ordinary acts of heroism and sacrifice too often overlooked by history.   <\/span>\n  <a But when word comes down from on high that the case is to be dropped immediately, Landsman soon finds himself contending with all the powerful forces of faith, obsession, hopefulness, evil, and <span id="freeTextContainer10815383728618636574">Jo Walton writes science fiction and fantasy novels and reads a lot and eats great food. It worries her slightly that this is so exactly what she always wanted to do when she grew up. She comes from Wales, but lives in Montreal.<br/></span>
